You MUST use the Sourcegraph MCP tools (keyword_search, finder, list_files, read_file, go_to_definition, find_references, deepsearch, deepsearch_read) to explore and understand this codebase. You are REQUIRED to use these tools extensively — they are your primary means of code discovery.

Sourcegraph repository: `github.com/django/django` (use `repo:^github.com/django/django$` filter in keyword_search)

**REQUIRED WORKFLOW:**
1. FIRST use Sourcegraph tools (keyword_search, finder, list_files) to explore the codebase structure and find relevant code
2. Use go_to_definition and find_references to trace code relationships
3. After finding code via Sourcegraph, use Read to verify actual local contents before editing
4. If Sourcegraph shows different code than local, TRUST THE LOCAL CODE
5. Use `deepsearch` for complex architectural questions — it returns a polling link, NOT immediate results
6. After calling `deepsearch`, continue working with other tools for 30-60 seconds, then call `deepsearch_read` with the polling link
7. Retry `deepsearch_read` at least 3 times (Deep Search takes 50-120 seconds to complete)

See CLAUDE.md for the complete tool selection guide with detailed usage for each tool.

# Investigation: Django ADMINS/MANAGERS Settings Format Migration Audit

**Repository:** django/django
**Task Type:** Migration Audit (investigation only — no code changes)

## Scenario

Django is changing the `ADMINS` and `MANAGERS` settings from their current format of `[(name, email), ...]` tuples to simple `[email, ...]` string lists. This is tracked in ticket #36138. Before executing this migration, the team needs a comprehensive audit of all code that reads, validates, or processes these settings.

## Your Task

Produce a migration audit report at `/logs/agent/investigation.md`.

Your report MUST cover:
1. Every file that reads `settings.ADMINS` or `settings.MANAGERS`
2. Every file that validates or type-checks the settings format
3. Every file that unpacks the tuple format (e.g., `for name, email in settings.ADMINS`)
4. All documentation references that show the old tuple format
5. Test files that use the old format in fixtures or assertions
6. Third-party compatibility concerns (what breaks for users with old-format settings)
7. A migration checklist: each file that needs changes, what change is needed

## Output Requirements

Write your investigation report to `/logs/agent/investigation.md` with these sections:

```
# Investigation Report

## Summary
<1-2 sentence finding>

## Root Cause
<Core code paths that consume the tuple format>

## Evidence
<Code references with file paths and line numbers>

## Affected Components
<List of modules/packages impacted>

## Recommendation
<Migration checklist with specific files and changes needed>
```

## Constraints

- Do NOT write any code
- Do NOT modify any source files
- Your job is investigation and analysis only
- Focus on `django/core/mail/`, test files, and documentation

{"content":"1: =============================\n2: How to manage error reporting\n3: =============================\n4: \n5: When you're running a public site you should always turn off the\n6: :setting:`DEBUG` setting. That will make your server run much faster, and will\n7: also prevent malicious users from seeing details of your application that can\n8: be revealed by the error pages.\n9: \n10: However, running with :setting:`DEBUG` set to ``False`` means you'll never see\n11: errors generated by your site -- everyone will instead see your public error\n12: pages. You need to keep track of errors that occur in deployed sites, so Django\n13: can be configured to create reports with details about those errors.\n14: \n15: Email reports\n16: =============\n17: \n18: Server errors\n19: -------------\n20: \n21: When :setting:`DEBUG` is ``False``, Django will email the users listed in the\n22: :setting:`ADMINS` setting whenever your code raises an unhandled exception and\n23: results in an internal server error (strictly speaking, for any response with\n24: an HTTP status code of 500 or greater). This gives the administrators immediate\n25: notification of any errors. The :setting:`ADMINS` will get a description of the\n26: error, a complete Python traceback, and details about the HTTP request that\n27: caused the error.\n28: \n29: .. note::\n30: \n31:    In order to send email, Django requires a few settings telling it\n32:    how to connect to your mail server. At the very least, you'll need\n33:    to specify :setting:`EMAIL_HOST` and possibly\n34:    :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_USER` and :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD`,\n35:    though other settings may be also required depending on your mail\n36:    server's configuration. It should be a list of compiled\n81: regular expression objects. For example::\n82: \n83:     import re\n84: \n85:     IGNORABLE_404_URLS = [\n86:         re.compile(r\"\\.(php|cgi)$\"),\n87:         re.compile(r\"^/phpmyadmin/\"),\n88:     ]\n89: \n90: In this example, a 404 to any URL ending with ``.php`` or ``.cgi`` will *not*\n91: be reported.     79→class AdminEmailHandler(logging.Handler):
    80→    """An exception log handler that emails log entries to site admins.
    81→
    82→    If the request is passed as the first argument to the log record,
    83→    request data will be provided in the email report.
    84→    """
    85→
    86→    def __init__(self, include_html=False, email_backend=None, reporter_class=None):
    87→        super().__init__()
    88→        self.include_html = include_html
    89→        self.email_backend = email_backend
    90→        self.reporter_class = import_string(
    91→            reporter_class or settings.DEFAULT_EXCEPTION_REPORTER
    92→        )
    93→
    94→    def emit(self, record):
    95→        # Early return when no email will be sent.
    96→        if (
    97→            not settings.ADMINS
    98→            # Method not overridden.
    99→            and self.send_mail.__func__ is AdminEmailHandler.send_mail
   100→        ):
   101→            return
   102→        try:
   103→            request = record.request
   104→            subject = "%s (%s IP): %s" % (
   105→                record.levelname,
   106→                (
   107→                    "internal"
   108→                    if request.META.get("REMOTE_ADDR") in settings.INTERNAL_IPS
   109→                    else "EXTERNAL"
   110→                ),
   111→                record.getMessage(),
   112→            )
   113→        except Exception:
   114→            subject = "%s: %s" % (record.levelname, record.getMessage())
   115→            request = None
   116→        subject = self.format_subject(subject)
   117→
   118→        # Since we add a nicely formatted traceback on our own, create a copy
   119→        # of the log record without the exception data.
   120→

    43→.. setting:: ADMINS
    44→
    45→``ADMINS``
    46→----------
    47→
    48→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    49→
    50→A list of all the people who get code error notifications. When
    51→:setting:`DEBUG=False <DEBUG>` and :class:`~django.utils.log.AdminEmailHandler`
    52→is configured in :setting:`LOGGING` (done by default), Django emails these
    53→people the details of exceptions raised in the request/response cycle.
    54→
    55→Each item in the list should be a tuple of (Full name, email address). Example::
    56→
    57→    [("John", "john@example.com"), ("Mary", "mary@example.com")]
    58→
    59→.. setting:: ALLOWED_HOSTS

162-assistanttext-
The investigation report has been written to `/logs/agent/investigation.md`. Here's a summary of the findings:

**Scope of impact**: 6 source files, 5 test files, 5+ documentation files.

**Core processing**: Everything flows through a single function — `_send_server_message()` in `django/core/mail/__init__.py:122-147`. It validates the 2-tuple format (line 135) and extracts emails via `a[1]` (line 142). Both `mail_admins()` and `mail_managers()` delegate to it.

**Key findings**:
- The local codebase is in the **pre-migration state** — it still requires the old `[(name, email), ...]` tuple format
- The name field in tuples is **completely discarded** — only the email (`a[1]`) is ever used
- **18 test locations** across 5 files use the old tuple format in `@override_settings` decorators
- Documentation in `docs/ref/settings.txt` shows the old tuple format as the example
- The `AdminEmailHandler` in `django/utils/log.py` and `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` in `django/middleware/common.py` are indirect consumers (they call through `mail_admins`/`mail_managers`)

The report includes a detailed 22-item migration checklist with specific files, line numbers, and required changes.

That will make your server run much faster, and will\\n7: also prevent malicious users from seeing details of your application that can\\n8: be revealed by the error pages.\\n9: \\n10: However, running with :setting:`DEBUG` set to ``False`` means you'll never see\\n11: errors generated by your site -- everyone will instead see your public error\\n12: pages. You need to keep track of errors that occur in deployed sites, so Django\\n13: can be configured to create reports with details about those errors.\\n14: \\n15: Email reports\\n16: =============\\n17: \\n18: Server errors\\n19: -------------\\n20: \\n21: When :setting:`DEBUG` is ``False``, Django will email the users listed in the\\n22: :setting:`ADMINS` setting whenever your code raises an unhandled exception and\\n23: results in an internal server error (strictly speaking, for any response with\\n24: an HTTP status code of 500 or greater). This gives the administrators immediate\\n25: notification of any errors. The :setting:`ADMINS` will get a description of the\\n26: error, a complete Python traceback, and details about the HTTP request that\\n27: caused the error.\\n28: \\n29: .. note::\\n30: \\n31:    In order to send email, Django requires a few settings telling it\\n32:    how to connect to your mail server. At the very least, you'll need\\n33:    to specify :setting:`EMAIL_HOST` and possibly\\n34:    :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_USER` and :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD`,\\n35:    though other settings may be also required depending on your mail\\n36:    server's configuration. Consult :doc:`the Django settings\\n37:    documentation </ref/settings>` for a full list of email-related\\n38:    settings.\\n39: \\n40: By default, Django will send email from ``root@localhost``. However, some mail\\n41: providers reject all email from this address.     "content": "1: =============================\n2: How to manage error reporting\n3: =============================\n4: \n5: When you're running a public site you should always turn off the\n6: :setting:`DEBUG` setting. That will make your server run much faster, and will\n7: also prevent malicious users from seeing details of your application that can\n8: be revealed by the error pages.\n9: \n10: However, running with :setting:`DEBUG` set to ``False`` means you'll never see\n11: errors generated by your site -- everyone will instead see your public error\n12: pages. You need to keep track of errors that occur in deployed sites, so Django\n13: can be configured to create reports with details about those errors.\n14: \n15: Email reports\n16: =============\n17: \n18: Server errors\n19: -------------\n20: \n21: When :setting:`DEBUG` is ``False``, Django will email the users listed in the\n22: :setting:`ADMINS` setting whenever your code raises an unhandled exception and\n23: results in an internal server error (strictly speaking, for any response with\n24: an HTTP status code of 500 or greater). This gives the administrators immediate\n25: notification of any errors. The :setting:`ADMINS` will get a description of the\n26: error, a complete Python traceback, and details about the HTTP request that\n27: caused the error.\n28: \n29: .. note::\n30: \n31:    In order to send email, Django requires a few settings telling it\n32:    how to connect to your mail server. At the very least, you'll need\n33:    to specify :setting:`EMAIL_HOST` and possibly\n34:    :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_USER` and :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD`,\n35:    though other settings may be also required depending on your mail\n36:    server's configuration. Consult :doc:`the Django settings\n37:    documentation </ref/settings>` for a full list of email-related\n38:    settings.\n39: \n40: By default, Django will send email from ``root@localhost``. The core processing logic lives in a single function (`_send_server_message` in `django/core/mail/__init__.py`) which validates the tuple format and extracts email addresses via `a[1]`.\n\n## Root Cause\n\nThe tuple format is consumed by a single core function that validates and unpacks it:\n\n**`django/core/mail/__init__.py:122-147`** \u2014 `_send_server_message()`\n```python\ndef _send_server_message(*, setting_name, subject, message, html_message=None,\n                         fail_silently=False, connection=None):\n    recipients = getattr(settings, setting_name)\n    if not recipients:\n        return\n    if not all(isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2 for a in recipients):\n        raise ValueError(f\"The {setting_name} setting must be a list of 2-tuples.\")\n    mail = EmailMultiAlternatives(\n        subject=\"%s%s\" % (settings.EMAIL_SUBJECT_PREFIX, subject),\n        body=message,\n        from_email=settings.SERVER_EMAIL,\n        to=[a[1] for a in recipients],   # <-- extracts email from tuple\n        connection=connection,\n    )\n```\n\nBoth `mail_admins()` and `mail_managers()` delegate to this function, passing `setting_name=\"ADMINS\"` or `setting_name=\"MANAGERS\"` respectively.\n\n## Evidence\n\n### 1. 404 notifications\n4. **`django.core.management.commands.sendtestemail`** \u2014 Management command calls `mail_admins()`/`mail_managers()`\n5. **`django.conf.global_settings`** \u2014 Default values and format documentation comments\n6. **Test suites** \u2014 `tests/mail/`, `tests/logging_tests/`, `tests/view_tests/`, `tests/middleware/`\n7. **Documentation** \u2014 `docs/ref/settings.txt`, `docs/howto/error-reporting.txt`, `docs/topics/email.txt`, `docs/ref/logging.txt`, `docs/howto/deployment/checklist.txt`\n\n## Third-Party Compatibility Concerns\n\n1. **All existing Django projects** that have `ADMINS` or `MANAGERS` configured in their `settings.py` use the tuple format (e.g., `ADMINS = [(\"Admin Name\", \"admin@example.com\")]`). The migration will break their configuration unless a deprecation shim is provided.\n\n2. **Third-party packages** that call `mail_admins()` or `mail_managers()` are **not affected** since they don't directly consume the setting \u2014 they go through the Django API. However, packages that directly read `settings.ADMINS` or `settings.MANAGERS` and unpack tuples (e.g., `for name, email in settings.ADMINS`) will break.\n\n3. **The `name` field is discarded**: Currently, the name portion of the tuple is completely ignored by `_send_server_message()` (it only uses `a[1]`). Users who relied on the name for display purposes elsewhere will lose that data.\n\n4. **Backward compatibility**: The Sourcegraph version of the code (default branch) already has the deprecation shim in place \u2014 it checks for the old format, issues a `RemovedInDjango70Warning`, and extracts emails from tuples. The local checkout is the pre-migration state.\n\n## Recommendation\n\n### Migration Checklist\n\nEach file that needs changes, ordered by priority:\n\n#### Core Source Files (must change)\n\n| # | File | Line(s) | Change Needed |\n|---|------|---------|---------------|\n| 1 | `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 122-147 | **Primary change**: Modify `_send_server_message()` to accept plain email strings instead of 2-tuples. # Investigation Report

## Summary

The `ADMINS` and `MANAGERS` settings currently require a list of `(name, email)` 2-tuples. The migration to plain `[email, ...]` string lists (ticket #36138) affects **6 source files**, **5 test files**, and **5 documentation files**. The core processing logic lives in a single function (`_send_server_message` in `django/core/mail/__init__.py`) which validates the tuple format and extracts email addresses via `a[1]`.

## Root Cause

The tuple format is consumed by a single core function that validates and unpacks it:

**`django/core/mail/__init__.py:122-147`** — `_send_server_message()`
```python
def _send_server_message(*, setting_name, subject, message, html_message=None,
                         fail_silently=False, connection=None):
    recipients = getattr(settings, setting_name)
    if not recipients:
        return
    if not all(isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2 for a in recipients):
        raise ValueError(f"The {setting_name} setting must be a list of 2-tuples.")
    mail = EmailMultiAlternatives(
        subject="%s%s" % (settings.EMAIL_SUBJECT_PREFIX, subject),
        body=message,
        from_email=settings.SERVER_EMAIL,
        to=[a[1] for a in recipients],   # <-- extracts email from tuple
        connection=connection,
    )
```

Both `mail_admins()` and `mail_managers()` delegate to this function, passing `setting_name="ADMINS"` or `setting_name="MANAGERS"` respectively.

## Evidence

### 1. Files That Read `settings.ADMINS` or `settings.MANAGERS`

| File | Line(s) | How It Reads the Setting |
|------|---------|--------------------------|
| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 131 | `getattr(settings, setting_name)` — reads ADMINS or MANAGERS dynamically |
| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 135 | Validates 
... (truncated)
